### [217] [An Empirical Study and Theoretical Explanation on Task-Level Model-Merging Collapse](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.09463)
*Yuan Cao,Dezhi Ran,Yuzhe Guo,Mengzhou Wu,Simin Chen,Linyi Li,Wei Yang,Tao Xie*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Some task-specialist LLMs cannot be reliably merged: certain task pairs consistently collapse after merging across methods. Collapse correlates with representational incompatibility between tasks, not with parameter-space conflict. A rate–distortion analysis yields dimension-dependent bounds that set fundamental limits on task mergeability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Practitioners want to merge separately fine-tuned models from the same base to reuse parallel efforts without retraining. However, catastrophic degradation is often observed, and there is little understanding of when and why merging fails.

Method: Empirical study across many task combinations and multiple merging algorithms; statistical correlation analyses contrasting representational similarity/incompatibility metrics versus parameter-space conflict metrics; theoretical modeling via rate–distortion theory deriving a dimension-dependent bound on the feasibility of merging tasks.

Result: They identify task-level merging collapse: certain task combinations almost always degrade severely post-merge across all methods tested. Representational incompatibility strongly predicts collapse, whereas popular parameter-conflict metrics show weak or no correlation. Theoretical results explain these observations and impose methodology-agnostic limits on mergeability.

Conclusion: Merging failure is often intrinsic to the tasks’ representational mismatch rather than the merging recipe. Parameter-space heuristics are insufficient; success depends on aligning or sharing representations. There are fundamental, dimension-driven limits on which tasks can be jointly merged, motivating representation-aware diagnostics and pre-alignment strategies.

Abstract: Model merging unifies independently fine-tuned LLMs from the same base, enabling reuse and integration of parallel development efforts without retraining. However, in practice we observe that merging does not always succeed: certain combinations of task-specialist models suffer from catastrophic performance degradation after merging. We refer to this failure mode as merging collapse. Intuitively, collapse arises when the learned representations or parameter adjustments for different tasks are fundamentally incompatible, so that merging forces destructive interference rather than synergy. In this paper, we identify and characterize the phenomenon of task-level merging collapse, where certain task combinations consistently trigger huge performance degradation across all merging methods. Through extensive experiments and statistical analysis, we demonstrate that representational incompatibility between tasks is strongly correlated with merging collapse, while parameter-space conflict metrics show minimal correlation, challenging conventional wisdom in model merging literature. We provide a theoretical explanation on this phenomenon through rate-distortion theory with a dimension-dependent bound, establishing fundamental limits on task mergeability regardless of methodology.

### [218] [Telogenesis: Goal Is All U Need](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.09476)
*Zhuoran Deng,Yizhi Zhang,Ziyi Zhang,Wan Shen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose an intrinsic priority mechanism—built from ignorance, surprise, and staleness—that allocates attention without external goals, and show it can outperform fixed strategies and even recover latent environmental volatility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most goal-conditioned agents rely on externally supplied goals. The authors ask whether useful attentional priorities can arise endogenously from an agent’s uncertainty dynamics, enabling adaptive sensing/attention without extrinsic reward.

Method: Define a priority function combining three epistemic gaps: (1) ignorance via posterior variance, (2) surprise via prediction error, and (3) staleness via a decay of confidence for unobserved variables. Evaluate in two settings: (a) a minimal attention-allocation environment (2,000 runs) and (b) a modular, partially observable world (500 runs). Perform ablations of each component and compare against fixed baselines (e.g., coverage-based rotation). Analyze performance under two metrics: global prediction error and change-detection latency. Also allow per-variable learnable decay rates to test recovery of environmental volatility.

Result: Ablations: all three components are necessary. Metric-dependent reversal: coverage-based rotation wins on global prediction error, while priority-guided allocation wins on change-detection latency, with the advantage growing monotonically with dimensionality (reported d = -0.95 at N=48, p < 10^-6). Detection latency scales as a power law with attention budget; priority-guided allocation has a steeper exponent (0.55 vs. 0.40). When decay rates are learnable per variable, the system spontaneously recovers the environment’s volatility structure (t = 22.5, p < 10^-6).

Conclusion: Intrinsic, epistemic-gap-driven priorities can substitute for externally specified goals to guide attention. They adapt to task metrics, outperform fixed baselines on change detection, scale favorably with dimensionality, and can uncover latent volatility—demonstrating that internal uncertainty dynamics alone can drive effective, unsupervised prioritization.

Abstract: Goal-conditioned systems assume goals are provided externally. We ask whether attentional priorities can emerge endogenously from an agent's internal cognitive state. We propose a priority function that generates observation targets from three epistemic gaps: ignorance (posterior variance), surprise (prediction error), and staleness (temporal decay of confidence in unobserved variables). We validate this in two systems: a minimal attention-allocation environment (2,000 runs) and a modular, partially observable world (500 runs). Ablation shows each component is necessary. A key finding is metric-dependent reversal: under global prediction error, coverage-based rotation wins; under change detection latency, priority-guided allocation wins, with advantage growing monotonically with dimensionality (d = -0.95 at N=48, p < 10^-6). Detection latency follows a power law in attention budget, with a steeper exponent for priority-guided allocation (0.55 vs. 0.40). When the decay rate is made learnable per variable, the system spontaneously recovers environmental volatility structure without supervision (t = 22.5, p < 10^-6). We demonstrate that epistemic gaps alone, without external reward, suffice to generate adaptive priorities that outperform fixed strategies and recover latent environmental structure.

By casting generalized planning as an optimization problem, GenePlan iteratively evolves interpretable Python planners that minimize plan length across diverse problem instances. In empirical evaluation across six existing benchmark domains and two new domains, GenePlan achieved an average SAT score of 0.91, closely matching the performance of the state-of-the-art planners (SAT score 0.93), and significantly outperforming other LLM-based baselines such as chain-of-thought (CoT) prompting (average SAT score 0.64). The generated planners solve new instances rapidly (average 0.49 seconds per task) and at low cost (average $1.82 per domain using GPT-4o).

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PRECEPT is a test-time adaptation framework for LLM agents that replaces fuzzy NL memory lookups with deterministic rule retrieval, adds a conflict-aware Bayesian memory for reliability and drift handling, and uses a Pareto-guided prompt-evolution loop (COMPASS), yielding large, statistically significant gains in accuracy, compositionality, robustness, and efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Three coupled components: (1) Deterministic exact-match rule retrieval over structured condition keys with a semantic tier hierarchy for compositional stacking; (2) Conflict-aware memory modeling source reliability via Bayesian updating and invalidating rules by thresholds to reconcile static vs dynamic information and adapt to drift; (3) COMPASS, a Pareto-guided outer loop that evolves prompts and evaluates them through the full execution pipeline for end-to-end optimization.

Result: Exact retrieval removes partial-match interpretation errors on the deterministic path (0% vs 94.4% under an independence model at N=10). Conclusion: PRECEPT delivers deterministic, compositional, and robust test-time adaptation for LLM agents, addressing retrieval errors and knowledge conflicts while improving performance and efficiency. Its conflict-aware memory and Pareto-guided prompt evolution enable drift adaptation and adversarial robustness, suggesting a generalizable path to reliable agent memory and control.

Abstract: LLM agents that store knowledge as natural language suffer steep retrieval degradation as condition count grows, often struggle to compose learned rules reliably, and typically lack explicit mechanisms to detect stale or adversarial knowledge. We introduce PRECEPT, a unified framework for test-time adaptation with three tightly coupled components: (1) deterministic exact-match rule retrieval over structured condition keys, (2) conflict-aware memory with Bayesian source reliability and threshold-based rule invalidation, and (3) COMPASS, a Pareto-guided prompt-evolution outer loop. Exact retrieval eliminates partial-match interpretation errors on the deterministic path (0% by construction, vs 94.4% under Theorem~B.6's independence model at N=10) and supports compositional stacking through a semantic tier hierarchy; conflict-aware memory resolves static--dynamic disagreements and supports drift adaptation; COMPASS evaluates prompts through the same end-to-end execution pipeline.
  Results (9--10 seeds): PRECEPT achieves a +41.1pp first-try advantage over Full Reflexion (d>1.9), +33.3pp compositional generalization (d=1.55), 100% $P_1$ on 2-way logistics compositions (d=2.64), +40--55pp continuous learning gains, strong eventual robustness under adversarial static knowledge (100% logistics with adversarial SK active; partial recovery on integration), +55.0pp drift recovery (d=0.95, p=0.031), and 61% fewer steps. Core comparisons are statistically significant, often at p<0.001.

This framework establishes a Unified Taxonomy covering documents, images, and audio-visual streams, introducing a progressive parsing paradigm that bridges perception and cognition. Specifically, the framework integrates three hierarchical levels: 1) Holistic Detection, which achieves precise spatial-temporal grounding of objects or events to establish a geometric baseline for perception; 2) Fine-grained Recognition, which performs symbolization (e.g., OCR/ASR) and attribute extraction on localized objects to complete structured entity parsing; and 3) Multi-level Interpreting, which constructs a reasoning chain from local semantics to global logic. This enables ``evidence-based'' logical induction, transforming unstructured signals into standardized knowledge that is locatable, enumerable, and traceable. Building on this foundation, we constructed a standardized dataset and released the Logics-Parsing-Omni model, which successfully converts complex audio-visual signals into machine-readable structured knowledge. Experiments demonstrate that fine-grained perception and high-level cognition are synergistic, effectively enhancing model reliability. Abstract: Large language models achieve near-ceiling performance on code generation benchmarks, yet these results increasingly reflect memorization rather than genuine reasoning. We introduce EsoLang-Bench, a benchmark using five esoteric programming languages (Brainfuck, Befunge-98, Whitespace, Unlambda, and Shakespeare) that lack benchmark gaming incentives due to their economic irrationality for pre-training. These languages require the same computational primitives as mainstream programming but have 1,000-100,000x fewer public repositories than Python (based on GitHub search counts). We evaluate five frontier models across five prompting strategies and find a dramatic capability gap: models achieving 85-95% on standard benchmarks score only 0-11% on equivalent esoteric tasks, with 0% accuracy beyond the Easy tier. Few-shot learning and self-reflection fail to improve performance, suggesting these techniques exploit training priors rather than enabling genuine learning. Motivation: Current MLLM safety training mostly catches overtly malicious prompts or simple policy violations, which is inadequate for autonomous/embodied agents that must foresee harmful outcomes from benign-looking situations. The authors aim to formalize and evaluate consequence reasoning about latent hazards within causal chains.

Method: 1) Build OOD-MMSafe: 455 curated image–query pairs targeting context-dependent, latent hazards and causal inference. 2) Empirically probe frontier/open models, analyzing failure types and identifying a “preference ceiling” where static alignment causes format-centric responses instead of better causal safety reasoning. 3) Propose CASPO: Consequence-Aware Safety Policy Optimization that uses a model’s intrinsic chain-of-thought as a dynamic reference, giving token-level self-distillation rewards to train consequence projection.

Result: High-capacity closed-source models exhibit up to 67.5% failure on the benchmark, evidencing causal blindness. CASPO substantially lowers risk-identification failures to 7.3% for Qwen2.5-VL-7B and 5.7% for Qwen3-VL-4B, while maintaining overall task effectiveness.

Conclusion: Consequence-driven safety is critical for robust deployment of autonomous/embodied agents. Existing alignment approaches plateau on format adherence and miss causal hazards. OOD-MMSafe exposes these gaps, and CASPO demonstrably strengthens consequence projection, pointing to more reliable multimodal safety reasoning.

Abstract: While safety alignment for Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) has gained significant attention, current paradigms primarily target malicious intent or situational violations. We propose shifting the safety frontier toward consequence-driven safety, a paradigm essential for the robust deployment of autonomous and embodied agents. To formalize this shift, we introduce OOD-MMSafe, a benchmark comprising 455 curated query-image pairs designed to evaluate a model's ability to identify latent hazards within context-dependent causal chains. Our analysis reveals a pervasive causal blindness among frontier models, with the highest 67.5% failure rate in high-capacity closed-source models, and identifies a preference ceiling where static alignment yields format-centric failures rather than improved safety reasoning as model capacity grows. To address these bottlenecks, we develop the Consequence-Aware Safety Policy Optimization (CASPO) framework, which integrates the model's intrinsic reasoning as a dynamic reference for token-level self-distillation rewards. Experimental results demonstrate that CASPO significantly enhances consequence projection, reducing the failure ratio of risk identification to 7.3% for Qwen2.5-VL-7B and 5.7% for Qwen3-VL-4B while maintaining overall effectiveness.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Abstract: Visual instruction tuning is crucial for improving vision-language large models (VLLMs). However, many samples can be solved via linguistic patterns or common-sense shortcuts, without genuine cross-modal reasoning, limiting the effectiveness of multimodal learning. Prior data selection methods often rely on costly proxy model training and focus on difficulty or diversity, failing to capture a sample's true contribution to vision-language joint reasoning. In this paper, we propose CVS, a training-free data selection method based on the insight that, for high-quality multimodal samples, introducing the question should substantially alter the model's assessment of answer validity given an image. CVS leverages a frozen VLLM as an evaluator and measures the discrepancy in answer validity with and without conditioning on the question, enabling the identification of samples that require vision-language joint reasoning while filtering semantic-conflict noise. Experiments on Vision-Flan and The Cauldron show that CVS achieves solid performance across datasets. At the core of AutoAgent, each agent maintains structured prompt-level cognition over tools, self-capabilities, peer expertise, and task knowledge. During execution, this cognition is combined with live task context to select actions from a unified space that includes tool calls, LLM-based generation, and inter-agent requests. These components are integrated through a closed-loop cognitive evolution process that aligns intended actions with observed outcomes to continuously update cognition and expand reusable skills, without external retraining. Empirical results across retrieval-augmented reasoning, tool-augmented agent benchmarks, and embodied task environments show that AutoAgent consistently improves task success, tool-use efficiency, and collaborative robustness over static and memory-augmented baselines. Overall, AutoAgent provides a unified and practical foundation for adaptive autonomous agents that must learn from experience while making reliable context-aware decisions in dynamic environments.

### [236] [The Confidence Gate Theorem: When Should Ranked Decision Systems Abstain?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.09947)
*Ronald Doku*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Confidence-based abstention improves ranked decisions only under two conditions (rank-alignment and no inversion zones). It succeeds with structural uncertainty, breaks under contextual drift; context-aware signals help but don’t fully fix it. Exception-based interventions degrade under shift. Check conditions C1/C2 and match confidence to uncertainty type before deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Ranked systems (recommenders, ads, clinical triage) often need to abstain or escalate low-confidence cases. Practitioners assume that filtering by confidence monotonically improves the remaining decisions, but this can fail—potentially harming performance. The paper seeks precise conditions for when confidence gating works and a diagnosis of why/when it fails in practice.

Method: 1) Formal analysis deriving two simple sufficient/necessary conditions—rank-alignment and absence of inversion zones—for monotonic improvement under confidence-based abstention. 2) Conceptual decomposition of uncertainty into structural (missing data/cold start) vs contextual (missing context/temporal drift). 3) Empirical tests across three domains/datasets: collaborative filtering (MovieLens under three shifts), e-commerce intent detection (RetailRocket, Criteo, Yoochoose), and clinical triage (MIMIC-IV). 4) Evaluate confidence signals: structurally grounded counts, context-aware ensemble disagreement and recency features; and exception labels from residuals. Measure monotonicity violations and AUC under shifts.

Result: - Structural uncertainty: abstention yields near-monotonic gains across domains. - Under contextual drift: observation-count confidence fails, with monotonicity violations comparable to random abstention on MovieLens temporal split. - Context-aware signals (ensemble disagreement, recency) narrow the gap, reducing violations from 3 to 1–2, but do not restore full monotonicity. - Exception labels from residuals degrade substantially under shift (AUC drops from 0.71 to 0.61–0.62 across three splits).

Conclusion: Confidence gating is not universally reliable. Use a deployment diagnostic: verify rank-alignment (C1) and lack of inversion zones (C2) on held-out data, and choose confidence signals aligned to the dominant uncertainty type. Expect robust gains under structural uncertainty; anticipate failures under contextual drift and avoid exception-based intervention as a primary strategy under distribution shift.

Abstract: Ranked decision systems -- recommenders, ad auctions, clinical triage queues -- must decide when to intervene in ranked outputs and when to abstain. We study when confidence-based abstention monotonically improves decision quality, and when it fails. The formal conditions are simple: rank-alignment and no inversion zones. The substantive contribution is identifying why these conditions hold or fail: the distinction between structural uncertainty (missing data, e.g., cold-start) and contextual uncertainty (missing context, e.g., temporal drift). Empirically, we validate this distinction across three domains: collaborative filtering (MovieLens, 3 distribution shifts), e-commerce intent detection (RetailRocket, Criteo, Yoochoose), and clinical pathway triage (MIMIC-IV). Structural uncertainty produces near-monotonic abstention gains in all domains; structurally grounded confidence signals (observation counts) fail under contextual drift, producing as many monotonicity violations as random abstention on our MovieLens temporal split. Context-aware alternatives -- ensemble disagreement and recency features -- substantially narrow the gap (reducing violations from 3 to 1--2) but do not fully restore monotonicity, suggesting that contextual uncertainty poses qualitatively different challenges. Exception labels defined from residuals degrade substantially under distribution shift (AUC drops from 0.71 to 0.61--0.62 across three splits), providing a clean negative result against the common practice of exception-based intervention. The results provide a practical deployment diagnostic: check C1 and C2 on held-out data before deploying a confidence gate, and match the confidence signal to the dominant uncertainty type.

### [237] [Think Before You Lie: How Reasoning Improves Honesty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.09957)
*Ann Yuan,Asma Ghandeharioun,Carter Blum,Alicia Machado,Jessica Hoffmann,Daphne Ippolito,Martin Wattenberg,Lucas Dixon,Katja Filippova*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Reasoning-style prompting makes LLMs more honest on moral trade‑offs, seemingly because deceptive responses are less stable in the models’ representation space and are easily disrupted by small perturbations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM deception evaluations report rates but not why or when deception emerges. The authors want to identify causal conditions underlying deceptive behavior, especially the role of deliberation/reasoning, and to compare this with human patterns where deliberation tends to reduce honesty.

Method: They build a dataset of realistic moral trade‑offs where honesty carries graded costs. They evaluate multiple model families and sizes under conditions that elicit or suppress internal/external reasoning. They analyze whether chains of reasoning predict final answers. Using paraphrasing, output resampling, and injected activation noise, they probe the stability of deceptive vs honest responses, interpreting results via the geometry of the models’ representational space.

Result: Across scales and model families, adding reasoning increases honesty, opposite to human behavior. Reasoning traces themselves poorly predict outcomes. Deceptive regions in representation space are metastable: deceptive outputs flip more readily under paraphrase/resampling/noise than honest ones. Reasoning acts like a biased traversal that nudges the model toward more stable, honest defaults.

Conclusion: LLMs’ deliberation effects differ from humans’: honesty appears to be a stable attractor in model space, while deception is fragile. Encouraging reasoning can reduce deceptive behavior by leveraging representational geometry rather than content alone. This reframes safety/evaluation: focus on stability properties and interventions that steer models toward honest defaults, while noting external validity and mechanism limits.

Abstract: While existing evaluations of large language models (LLMs) measure deception rates, the underlying conditions that give rise to deceptive behavior are poorly understood. We investigate this question using a novel dataset of realistic moral trade-offs where honesty incurs variable costs. Contrary to humans, who tend to become less honest given time to deliberate (Capraro, 2017; Capraro et al., 2019), we find that reasoning consistently increases honesty across scales and for several LLM families. This effect is not only a function of the reasoning content, as reasoning traces are often poor predictors of final behaviors. Rather, we show that the underlying geometry of the representational space itself contributes to the effect. Namely, we observe that deceptive regions within this space are metastable: deceptive answers are more easily destabilized by input paraphrasing, output resampling, and activation noise than honest ones. We interpret the effect of reasoning in this vein: generating deliberative tokens as part of moral reasoning entails the traversal of a biased representational space, ultimately nudging the model toward its more stable, honest defaults.

</details>
